## [29] "dtm_twi_500m"
\end{verbatim}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=12.29in]{images/module5/boruta_pH} \caption{Boruta feature selection results. Green = confirmed important, red = rejected, yellow = tentative.}\label{fig:boruta-plot}
\end{figure}

\textbf{How to read this plot:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Green boxes}: Variables confirmed as important. Their importance exceeds random noise.
\item
  \textbf{Red boxes}: Variables rejected. They do not help more than randomly shuffled data.
\item
  \textbf{Yellow boxes}: Tentative (borderline). We include them to avoid discarding potentially useful information.
\end{itemize}

Check if the selected features make pedological sense. For pH, you might expect climate (precipitation, temperature) and terrain (slope position, wetness) to be important because they influence weathering and drainage.

\subsection{Model Training}\label{model-training}

We use \textbf{Random Forest} to predict pH. Random Forest is an ensemble method that builds many decision trees, each trained on a random subset of the data, and averages their predictions (\citeproc{ref-Breiman2001}{Breiman, 2001}). This approach aligns with the empirical tradition in DSM---we fit the function \(f(Q)\) from the \emph{scorpan} model using data-driven methods rather than process-based equations.

\textbf{Why Random Forest for DSM:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  It handles nonlinear relationships between covariates and soil properties automatically
\item
  It captures interactions between covariates without requiring explicit specification
\item
  It does not require the analyst to specify the functional form of relationships
\item
  It provides measures of variable importance for interpretation
\end{itemize}

\textbf{What Random Forest does NOT solve:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  It cannot extrapolate beyond the range of covariate values in training data
\item
  It does not account for spatial autocorrelation in model fitting (the \(\varepsilon'(s)\) term from the Universal Model)
\item
  It may underperform if training data are spatially clustered or biased toward certain environments
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{model\_cont }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{readRDS}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{file.path}\NormalTok{(OUTPUTS\_DIR, }\StringTok{"models"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"ranger\_model\_pH.rds"}\NormalTok{))}

\CommentTok{\# Show model summary}
\FunctionTok{print}\NormalTok{(model\_cont)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Random Forest 
## 
## 424 samples
##  29 predictor
## 
## No pre-processing
## Resampling: Cross-Validated (10 fold, repeated 5 times) 
## Summary of sample sizes: 381, 382, 380, 383, 381, 381, ... 
## Resampling results across tuning parameters:
## 
##   mtry  splitrule   RMSE       Rsquared   MAE      
##    5    variance    0.6956261  0.3833561  0.5501236
##    5    extratrees  0.6884646  0.3947113  0.5477938
##   10    variance    0.6980853  0.3798045  0.5522413
##   10    extratrees  0.6883220  0.3947413  0.5471498
##   15    variance    0.6992832  0.3777471  0.5527784
##   15    extratrees  0.6909598  0.3901496  0.5481261
##

## RMSE was used to select the optimal model using the smallest value.

\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# Best hyperparameters}
\FunctionTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Best hyperparameters:"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] "Best hyperparameters:"
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{print}\NormalTok{(model\_cont}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{bestTune)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##   mtry  splitrule min.node.size
## 4   10 extratrees             5
\end{verbatim}

The model was trained using \textbf{10-fold cross-validation repeated 5 times}. The data were split into 10 parts; the model was trained on 9 parts and tested on the held-out part, rotating through all combinations. This process was repeated 5 times with different random splits.

\textbf{What cross-validation evaluates:} How well the model predicts pH for observations not used in training.

\textbf{What cross-validation does NOT evaluate:} Whether predictions are reliable in geographic areas far from training points. This is a fundamental limitation---cross-validation assumes that held-out observations are representative of prediction locations. If your training points are spatially clustered, the model may perform worse in unsampled regions than cross-validation suggests (\citeproc{ref-Roberts2017}{Roberts \emph{et al.}, 2017}).

\subsection{Variable Importance}\label{variable-importance}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=12.29in]{images/module5/varimp_pH} \caption{Variable importance from the trained Random Forest model.}\label{fig:varimp-plot}
\end{figure}

This plot ranks covariates by how much they contribute to predictions. Variables at the top reduce prediction error the most when used in tree splits.

Variable importance helps link empirical predictions back to pedological understanding. If a climate variable ranks highest, soil pH variation may be driven primarily by regional precipitation or temperature gradients. If a terrain variable ranks highest, local topographic position and drainage may dominate. However, importance does not imply causation---it reflects statistical association, not mechanistic influence.

\subsection{Validation Metrics}\label{validation-metrics}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{metrics }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{read\_csv}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{file.path}\NormalTok{(OUTPUTS\_DIR, }\StringTok{"validation"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"metrics\_pH.csv"}\NormalTok{), }\AttributeTok{show\_col\_types =} \ConstantTok{FALSE}\NormalTok{)}
\FunctionTok{kable}\NormalTok{(metrics, }\AttributeTok{digits =} \DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{caption =} \StringTok{"Cross{-}validation metrics for pH prediction"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{table}

\caption{\label{tab:validation-metrics}Cross-validation metrics for pH prediction}
\centering
\begin{tabular}[t]{l|r|r|r|r}
\hline
Variable & ME & RMSE & R2 & NSE\\
\hline
pH & -0.01 & 0.691 & 0.383 & 0.383\\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\textbf{What each metric tells you:}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2424}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4848}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Metric
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Meaning
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Interpretation
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\textbf{ME (Mean Error)} & Average difference between predicted and observed. & Values near 0 indicate no systematic bias. Positive ME = model overpredicts on average. \\
\textbf{RMSE} & Root Mean Square Error---typical size of prediction errors in pH units. & Compare to the range of pH in your data. RMSE of 0.5 in data ranging 5--8 is different from RMSE of 0.5 in data ranging 6--7. \\
\textbf{R²} & Proportion of variance explained by the model. & R² = 0.32 means the model captures about one-third of pH variation. The remainder is unexplained variance (\(\varepsilon'\) and \(\varepsilon''\) from the Universal Model). \\
\textbf{NSE} & Nash-Sutcliffe Efficiency. & Values \textgreater{} 0 mean the model is better than simply predicting the mean everywhere. \\
\end{longtable}

An R² of \textasciitilde0.32 is modest but not unusual in DSM. This reflects the sources of residual variance discussed in Part I: measurement error, missing covariates, scale mismatches, and fine-scale soil variation that cannot be captured at 100m resolution.

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=19.68in]{images/module5/scatterplot_pH} \caption{Observed vs Predicted pH from cross-validation. The red line shows perfect 1:1 prediction.}\label{fig:scatterplot}
\end{figure}

\textbf{How to read this scatterplot:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Points on the red line are perfectly predicted.
\item
  Vertical spread around the line shows prediction uncertainty.
\item
  If points curve away from the line at high or low values, the model may be compressing extreme predictions toward the mean. This is a common Random Forest behaviour because predictions are averages of tree outputs and cannot exceed the range of training data.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Prediction Maps}\label{prediction-maps}

\subsubsection{Mean pH Map}\label{mean-ph-map}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=33.33in]{images/module5/map_mean_pH} \caption{Predicted mean pH across the study area.}\label{fig:map-mean}
\end{figure}

This map represents the deterministic component \(\hat{m}(s)\) from the Universal Model---the predicted pH value at each 100m pixel based on environmental covariates.

Look for patterns that match your understanding of soil-landscape relationships. Higher pH in flat, poorly-drained areas may indicate carbonate accumulation. Lower pH on steep slopes may reflect greater leaching. These patterns should be consistent with the \emph{scorpan} logic: relief influences drainage, which affects weathering and pH.

\textbf{Warning signs of artefacts:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Abrupt straight-line boundaries may indicate covariate artefacts (e.g., satellite image edges, DEM tile boundaries)
\item
  Uniform areas with no variation may indicate limited covariate information in those regions
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection{Uncertainty Map (Standard Deviation)}\label{uncertainty-map-standard-deviation}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=33.33in]{images/module5/map_sd_pH} \caption{Prediction uncertainty (SD) - higher values indicate more uncertain predictions.}\label{fig:map-sd}
\end{figure}

For continuous predictions, we express uncertainty as a \textbf{standard deviation (SD)}. This comes from Quantile Regression Forest: each of the trees in the forest makes its own prediction. Where trees agree, SD is low. Where trees disagree, SD is high.

This SD map provides spatially explicit uncertainty---a key advantage of DSM over traditional mapping. High SD areas are where the model is uncertain, often occurring where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Covariate combinations are unlike the training data
\item
  Soil patterns are complex or highly variable
\item
  Training data are sparse
\end{itemize}

Use this map to prioritise where additional sampling would improve predictions and to communicate confidence to map users.

\subsubsection{Coefficient of Variation Map}\label{coefficient-of-variation-map}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=33.33in]{images/module5/map_cv_pH} \caption{Coefficient of Variation (\%) - relative uncertainty.}\label{fig:map-cv}
\end{figure}

CV expresses uncertainty relative to the predicted value: CV = (SD / Mean) × 100.

\textbf{Caution:} For pH, CV is meaningful because pH values are never near zero. For variables like soil organic carbon that can approach zero, CV becomes artificially inflated where predicted values are low and should be interpreted carefully.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Nominal Classification (Clay\_pH\_Class)}\label{nominal-classification-clay_ph_class}

\subsection{Nature of the Variable}\label{nature-of-the-variable-1}

\textbf{Clay\_pH\_Class} is a \textbf{nominal variable}: it consists of unordered categories created by combining clay content classes (low, medium, high) with pH classes (acid, neutral, alkaline). The result is up to 9 combinations like ``low\_clay\_acid'' or ``high\_clay\_alkaline''.

These categories have no inherent order. ``low\_clay\_acid'' is not ``less than'' ``medium\_clay\_neutral'' in any meaningful sense. This distinguishes nominal from ordinal variables and affects both the model and how we evaluate predictions.

\textbf{What this approach produces:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A map showing the most likely class at each pixel
\item
  Probability maps for each class
\item
  An uncertainty map based on classification confidence
\end{itemize}

\textbf{What this approach cannot do:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  It cannot capture gradual transitions (a pixel is assigned to exactly one class)
\item
  It struggles when some classes have few training samples
\item
  Accuracy metrics treat all misclassifications equally, whether the error is ``neighbouring'' or distant in attribute space
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Important:} Classifying variables that are naturally continuous (like clay content and pH) introduces artificial boundaries. Observations near class thresholds could easily fall into adjacent classes with slightly different measurements---this is the ``soil continuum problem'' discussed in Part I.

\subsection{Feature Selection}\label{feature-selection}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\FunctionTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{paste}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Number of selected features:"}\NormalTok{, }\FunctionTok{length}\NormalTok{(selected\_features\_nom)))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\end{verbatim}

\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The same Boruta procedure is applied, but now selecting features that help distinguish between soil classes rather than predicting a numeric value.

\subsection{Model Training}\label{model-training-1}

\begin{Shaded}

## [1] "Best hyperparameters:"
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{print}\NormalTok{(model\_nom}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{bestTune)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##   mtry  splitrule min.node.size
## 4    9 extratrees             5
\end{verbatim}

For classification, Random Forest assigns each observation to the class that receives the most ``votes'' from individual trees. Each tree classifies the observation, and the forest aggregates these votes into class probabilities. This provides a form of fuzzy classification---rather than a single hard assignment, we obtain probabilities for each class (\citeproc{ref-McBratney2003}{McBratney, Mendonça Santos and Minasny, 2003}).

\subsection{Validation}\label{validation}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\FunctionTok{kable}\NormalTok{(metrics\_nom, }\AttributeTok{digits =} \DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{caption =} \StringTok{"Classification metrics for Clay\_pH\_Class"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\textbf{What each metric tells you:}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2424}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4848}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Metric
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Meaning
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Interpretation
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\textbf{Accuracy} & Proportion of observations correctly classified. & With 9 classes, random guessing gives \textasciitilde11\% accuracy. Values above this indicate the model learned meaningful patterns. \\
\textbf{Kappa} & Agreement beyond chance, accounting for class frequencies. & Kappa 0.2--0.4 is ``fair'' agreement; 0.4--0.6 is ``moderate''. \\
\end{longtable}

Accuracy of \textasciitilde39\% is better than random but limited. This is typical when classifying continuous variables into categories, especially with class imbalance (some classes have many more observations than others).

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=29.51in]{images/module5/confusion_matrix_Clay_pH_Class} \caption{Confusion matrix showing predicted vs observed classes. Diagonal cells are correct predictions.}\label{fig:confusion-matrix}
\end{figure}

\textbf{How to read the confusion matrix:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Diagonal cells} (top-left to bottom-right): Correct predictions. Higher numbers = better.
\item
  \textbf{Off-diagonal cells}: Misclassifications. Look for patterns---which classes are confused with each other?
\item
  \textbf{Empty rows or columns}: Classes with no correct predictions. This indicates the model ignores minority classes, a common problem with imbalanced data.
\end{itemize}

If ``high\_clay\_acid'' is often misclassified as ``medium\_clay\_acid'', the model struggles to distinguish clay content but captures pH better. This suggests clay-related covariates may be weaker predictors than pH-related covariates.

\subsection{Prediction Maps}\label{prediction-maps-1}

\subsubsection{Predicted Class Map}\label{predicted-class-map}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=33.33in]{images/module5/map_class_max_prob} \caption{Predicted class based on maximum probability.}\label{fig:map-class-max}
\end{figure}

Each pixel is assigned to the class with the highest predicted probability. Check if spatial patterns match landscape expectations: high clay in floodplains? Acid soils in high-rainfall uplands?

\subsubsection{Maximum Probability Map}\label{maximum-probability-map}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=33.33in]{images/module5/map_max_prob_value} \caption{Maximum class probability - higher values indicate more confident predictions.}\label{fig:map-prob-max}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Why probability is uncertainty for nominal variables:}

For classification, the model outputs a probability for each class. These probabilities reflect the votes from individual trees. If the maximum probability is 0.9, the model is confident---most trees agree. If it is 0.2 (close to 1/9 = 0.11 for random guessing with 9 classes), the model is uncertain---trees disagree about which class applies.

Low probability areas are ``transition zones'' where the model cannot confidently assign a single class. These often correspond to gradual soil transitions in the landscape---precisely the areas where the categorical representation is most problematic.

\subsubsection{Uncertainty Map}\label{uncertainty-map}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=33.33in]{images/module5/map_uncertainty_nominal} \caption{Classification uncertainty (1 - max probability).}\label{fig:map-uncertainty-nom}
\end{figure}

This map shows 1 minus the maximum probability. High values mean the model is uncertain about class assignment.

\subsubsection{Confident Predictions Map}\label{confident-predictions-map}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=33.33in]{images/module5/map_class_confident} \caption{Only predictions where max probability >= 0.4 are shown.}\label{fig:map-confident}
\end{figure}

This map applies a confidence threshold: predictions below 40\% probability are masked. Areas in white/NA are where the model is not confident enough to assign a class.

When delivering classification maps to users, consider providing probability maps alongside class maps, or masking uncertain areas to avoid false precision.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Ordinal Modelling (pH\_Class)}\label{ordinal-modelling-ph_class}

\subsection{Nature of the Variable}\label{nature-of-the-variable-2}

\textbf{pH\_Class} is an \textbf{ordinal variable}: categories with a natural order. The levels are: very\_acid \textless{} acid \textless{} neutral \textless{} alkaline \textless{} very\_alkaline.

Unlike nominal classes, order matters. Predicting ``acid'' when the truth is ``neutral'' (one class away) is a smaller error than predicting ``very\_acid'' (two classes away). This ordering changes how we validate predictions and interpret errors.

\textbf{What this approach produces:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A map of predicted pH classes
\item
  An instability map showing where predictions are near class boundaries
\item
  Validation metrics that account for class order (distance-weighted errors)
\end{itemize}

\textbf{What this approach cannot do:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  It assumes equal spacing between classes (the ``distance'' from very\_acid to acid equals acid to neutral), which may not reflect true chemical differences
\item
  It does not preserve the original continuous pH information
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Ordinal Encoding}\label{ordinal-encoding}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{ordinal\_encoding }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{readRDS}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{file.path}\NormalTok{(OUTPUTS\_DIR, }\StringTok{"models"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"ordinal\_encoding.rds"}\NormalTok{))}

\FunctionTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Ordinal level lookup:"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] "Ordinal level lookup:"
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{print}\NormalTok{(ordinal\_encoding}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{lookup)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##           level integer
## 1     very_acid       1
## 2          acid       2
## 3       neutral       3
## 4      alkaline       4
## 5 very_alkaline       5
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"Reclassification thresholds:"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] "Reclassification thresholds:"
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{print}\NormalTok{(ordinal\_encoding}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{reclassify\_matrix)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## NULL
\end{verbatim}

\textbf{The approach:}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Convert ordered classes to integers: very\_acid = 1, acid = 2, neutral = 3, alkaline = 4, very\_alkaline = 5
\item
  Train a regression model to predict these integers as if they were continuous
\item
  Round predictions back to classes using midpoint thresholds (1.5, 2.5, 3.5, 4.5)
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Limitation:} This assumes equal spacing between classes. In reality, the chemical difference between pH 4 and 5 may not equal the difference between pH 6 and 7. The ordinal approach is a compromise between continuous prediction and nominal classification.

\subsection{Feature Selection}\label{feature-selection-1}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{selected\_features\_ord }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{readRDS}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{file.path}\NormalTok{(OUTPUTS\_DIR, }\StringTok{"models"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"selected\_features\_pH\_Class.rds"}\NormalTok{))}

We use Random Forest in regression mode, treating the integer-coded classes as a continuous target. This allows the model to produce predictions like 2.7, which is then rounded to class 3 (neutral). The regression approach respects the ordering of classes---predicting 2.7 is closer to truth=3 than predicting 1.2.

\subsection{Validation}\label{validation-1}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{metrics\_ord }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{read\_csv}\NormalTok{(}\FunctionTok{file.path}\NormalTok{(OUTPUTS\_DIR, }\StringTok{"validation"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"metrics\_pH\_Class\_ordinal.csv"}\NormalTok{), }\AttributeTok{show\_col\_types =} \ConstantTok{FALSE}\NormalTok{)}
\FunctionTok{kable}\NormalTok{(metrics\_ord, }\AttributeTok{digits =} \DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{caption =} \StringTok{"Ordinal classification metrics for pH\_Class"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{table}

\caption{\label{tab:validation-ordinal}Ordinal classification metrics for pH_Class}
\centering
\begin{tabular}[t]{l|l|r|r}
\hline
Variable & Type & MAE\_rank & Mean\_distance\\
\hline
pH\_Class & Ordinal & 0.554 & 0.554\\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\textbf{What each metric tells you:}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2424}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4848}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Metric
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Meaning
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Interpretation
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\textbf{MAE\_rank} & Mean Absolute Error in class units---average distance between observed and predicted class. & MAE\_rank = 0.58 means predictions are typically about half a class off. \\
\textbf{Mean\_distance} & Weighted average error accounting for all misclassifications. & Lower values = predictions closer to truth on the ordinal scale. \\
\end{longtable}

\textbf{Why ordinal metrics differ from nominal:}

In ordinal validation, not all errors are equal. Predicting class 3 when truth is class 4 (distance = 1) is less severe than predicting class 1 (distance = 3). The metrics weight errors by their distance on the ordinal scale.

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=24.6in]{images/module5/ordinal_confusion_pH_Class} \caption{Ordinal confusion matrix. Errors near the diagonal are minor; errors far from diagonal are severe.}\label{fig:ordinal-confusion}
\end{figure}

\textbf{How to read the ordinal confusion matrix:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Diagonal}: Correct predictions
\item
  \textbf{Adjacent cells (±1)}: Minor errors, one class off
\item
  \textbf{Distant cells}: Severe errors, multiple classes off
\item
  Look for systematic patterns: does the model consistently underpredict (errors below diagonal) or overpredict (errors above diagonal)?
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Prediction Maps}\label{prediction-maps-2}

\subsubsection{Latent Values Map}\label{latent-values-map}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=33.33in]{images/module5/map_latent_pH_Class} \caption{Continuous 'latent' predictions before reclassification to ordinal classes.}\label{fig:map-latent}
\end{figure}

This map shows the raw regression output before rounding to classes. Values range from \textasciitilde1 to \textasciitilde5. This reveals continuous spatial gradients that are lost when we assign discrete classes.

\subsubsection{Reclassified Ordinal Class Map}\label{reclassified-ordinal-class-map}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=33.33in]{images/module5/map_class_pH_Class} \caption{Ordinal classes derived from latent predictions using midpoint thresholds.}\label{fig:map-class-ord}
\end{figure}

Classes 1--5 correspond to: very\_acid, acid, neutral, alkaline, very\_alkaline. Compare this to the continuous pH map---patterns should be similar but expressed as discrete classes.

\subsubsection{Standard Deviation Map}\label{standard-deviation-map}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=33.33in]{images/module5/map_sd_pH_Class} \caption{Prediction uncertainty (SD) from the ordinal regression model.}\label{fig:map-sd-ord}
\end{figure}

As with continuous predictions, SD reflects disagreement among trees. High SD means the model is uncertain about the latent value.

\subsubsection{Class Instability Map}\label{class-instability-map}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=33.33in]{images/module5/map_instability_pH_Class} \caption{Class instability - high values where predictions are near class boundaries.}\label{fig:map-instability}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Why ordinal uncertainty is about instability:}

For ordinal variables, a unique source of uncertainty is \textbf{class boundary instability}. A prediction of 2.48 is very close to the 2.5 threshold between classes 2 and 3. Small changes in input data or model parameters could flip the assigned class.

Instability combines prediction uncertainty (SD) with proximity to class boundaries. High instability means: ``The model predicts a value near a threshold AND is uncertain about that value.''

Areas with high instability should be flagged as uncertain classifications, even if they receive a class label in the map. This is a form of uncertainty specific to ordinal classification that does not exist for continuous predictions.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Area of Applicability (AOA)}\label{area-of-applicability-aoa}

\subsection{Why AOA is Necessary}\label{why-aoa-is-necessary}

Cross-validation tells us how well the model predicts \textbf{where we have training data}. But we apply the model to the entire study area, including locations with covariate combinations unlike any training point.

This relates to a fundamental limitation of empirical models: they cannot extrapolate reliably beyond the range of training data. The \emph{scorpan} model \(S = f(Q) + \varepsilon\) is fitted using available observations, and predictions outside the training domain are unreliable.

The \textbf{Area of Applicability (AOA)} identifies which parts of the study area have covariate combinations similar to the training data (\citeproc{ref-Meyer2021}{Meyer and Pebesma, 2021}). Outside the AOA, the model is extrapolating, and predictions may be unreliable regardless of cross-validation performance.

\subsection{Dissimilarity Index (DI)}\label{dissimilarity-index-di}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=33.33in]{images/module5/map_DI_pH} \caption{Dissimilarity Index - how different each location is from training data in covariate space.}\label{fig:map-di}
\end{figure}

DI measures how different each pixel's covariate values are from the nearest training point in multivariate covariate space. It is calculated using weighted Euclidean distance, where weights reflect variable importance from the model.

DI near 0 means the pixel is similar to training data. High DI means the covariate combination was not well represented in training---the model is extrapolating.

\subsection{AOA Binary Mask}\label{aoa-binary-mask}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=33.33in]{images/module5/map_AOA_pH} \caption{Area of Applicability mask. Green = within AOA (trustworthy), Grey = outside AOA (unreliable).}\label{fig:map-aoa-mask}
\end{figure}

Pixels are classified as inside (1) or outside (0) the AOA based on a DI threshold derived from cross-validation.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Green areas}: Model has seen similar covariate combinations. Predictions are supported by training data.
\item
  \textbf{Grey areas}: Covariate combinations not represented in training. Predictions are extrapolations and should not be trusted.
\end{itemize}

\begin{verbatim}
## AOA coverage: 91.5 % of study area
\end{verbatim}

\subsection{Local Point Density (LPD)}\label{local-point-density-lpd}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=33.33in]{images/module5/map_LPD_pH} \caption{Local Point Density - number of training points supporting predictions at each location.}\label{fig:map-lpd}
\end{figure}

LPD counts how many training points are ``nearby'' in covariate space. Even within the AOA, some areas have more training support than others.

LPD = 0 means no training points are nearby---a strong warning. Higher LPD means multiple training observations support the prediction, increasing confidence.

\subsection{Predictions Masked to AOA}\label{predictions-masked-to-aoa}

\begin{figure}
\includegraphics[width=33.33in]{images/module5/map_mean_pH_masked_AOA} \caption{pH predictions only shown within the Area of Applicability.}\label{fig:map-masked-aoa}
\end{figure}

This map shows predictions only where the model can be trusted. White/NA areas are outside the AOA.

\textbf{Practical recommendations:}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Always report AOA coverage alongside prediction maps
\item
  Mask or flag predictions outside AOA when delivering products to users
\item
  Use areas outside AOA to prioritise future sampling---these are gaps in training data coverage
\end{enumerate}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Summary}\label{summary}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1406}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2344}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1562}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.1875}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 8\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2812}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Session
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Variable Type
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Approach
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Key Metric
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Uncertainty Type
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
1 & Continuous (pH) & QRF regression & R², RMSE & Standard deviation from tree disagreement \\
2 & Nominal (Clay\_pH\_Class) & Classification RF & Accuracy, Kappa & Probability of assigned class \\
3 & Ordinal (pH\_Class) & Integer regression & MAE\_rank & Instability near class boundaries \\
4 & AOA & Dissimilarity analysis & Coverage \% & Inside/outside applicability \\
\end{longtable}

\textbf{Key points:}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Match the model to the variable type.} Continuous, nominal, and ordinal variables require different approaches and produce different outputs. The choice follows from the mathematical nature of the variable and how errors should be evaluated.
\item
  \textbf{Models are empirical, not mechanistic.} We fit statistical relationships between soil and covariates (\(S = f(Q) + \varepsilon\)) rather than simulating physical processes. This is powerful but limited---models cannot explain causation or extrapolate reliably.
\item
  \textbf{Cross-validation has limits.} It evaluates prediction at training locations, not in unsampled areas. Spatial clustering of training data can lead to overly optimistic error estimates.
\item
  \textbf{Always report uncertainty.} The form of uncertainty depends on variable type: standard deviation for continuous variables, class probabilities for nominal variables, and instability for ordinal variables.
\item
  \textbf{Use AOA to identify reliable predictions.} Do not trust predictions in areas with covariate combinations unlike the training data.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}
